do_single_rollout

Problem: Fifty ping-pong-balls are numbered 1, 2, 3, ..., 49, 50. A ball is chosen at random. What is the probability that the number on the selected ball is a multiple of 5 or 7 or both? Express your answer as a common fraction. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: There are 10 balls whose number is divisible by 5. The balls $7, 14, \ldots,49$ are multiples of 7. There are 7 of these. The ball 35 is the unique ball that is a multiple of both 5 and 7. In total, there are $10+7-1 = 16$ balls whose number is divisible by 5 or 7. The probability that a randomly selected ball will be one of these 16 is $\frac{16}{50} = \boxed{\frac{8}{25}}$.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

The ball 35 is the unique ball that is a multiple of both 5 and 7. In total, there are $10+7-1 = 16$ balls whose number is divisible by 5 or 7. The probability that a randomly selected ball will be one of these 16 is $\frac{16}{50} = \boxed{\frac{8}{25}}$.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

do_single_rollout

Problem: Segment $AB$ has midpoint $C$, and segment $BC$ has midpoint $D$. Semi-circles are constructed with diameters $\overline{AB}$ and $\overline{BC}$ to form the entire region shown. Segment $CP$ splits the region into two sections of equal area. What is the degree measure of angle $ACP$? Express your answer as a decimal to the nearest tenth. [asy] draw((0,0)--10dir(180),linewidth(2)); draw((0,0)--10dir(67.5),linewidth(2)); draw((0,0)--10dir(0),dashed); draw(10dir(180)..10dir(90)..10dir(0),linewidth(2)); draw((5,0)+5dir(180)..(5,0)+5dir(-90)..(5,0)+5dir(0),linewidth(2)); dot((0,0)); dot((5,0)); label("A",10dir(180),W); label("B",10dir(0),E); label("C",(0,0),SW); label("D",5dir(0),NE); label("P",10dir(67.5),NE); [/asy] Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{67.5}

Reference Answer: The semi-circle with diameter BC has radius $\frac{1}{2}$ that of the semi-circle with diameter AB, and thus, has $\frac{1}{4}$ of the area. (Area of a circle $= \pi \times r^2$ - thus, if $r$ is half as large, that will be squared in the process). Therefore, the sum of their areas represents $\frac{5}{8}$ of a circle with diameter AB, and since the line CP splits this area exactly in half, that area would be $\frac{5}{16}$ of a circle with diameter AB. Therefore, the degree measure of that sector is $360 \times \frac{5}{16} = \boxed{112.5}$

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Therefore, the sum of their areas represents $\frac{5}{8}$ of a circle with diameter AB, and since the line CP splits this area exactly in half, that area would be $\frac{5}{16}$ of a circle with diameter AB. Therefore, the degree measure of that sector is $360 \times \frac{5}{16} = \boxed{112.5}$

Problem: Calculate \(\lim \limits_{x \to 0} \ x^3\Big(1+2^2+3^2+\ldots+\Big[\frac{1}{\vert x \vert}\Big]^2\Big)\), where \([\cdot]\) represents the greatest integer function. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Therefore, multiple approaches confirm that the limit is 1/3. **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{1}{3}} </think> To find the limit \(\lim \limits_{x \to 0} \ x^3\Big(1+2^2+3^2+\ldots+\Big[\frac{1}{\vert x \vert}\Big]^2\Big)\), we start by noting that as \(x \to 0\), \(\frac{1}{|x|}\) becomes very large. Let \(n = \left[\frac{1}{|x|}\right]\), which is the greatest integer less than or equal to \(\frac{1}{|x|}\). The sum of squares from 1 to \(n\) is given by the formula: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \] Substituting \(n \approx \frac{1}{|x|}\) (since \(n\) is the integer part of \(\frac{1}{|x|}\) and the difference becomes negligible as \(x \to 0\)), we approximate the sum: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right)\left(\frac{1}{|x|} + 1\right)\left(2\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right) + 1\right)}{6} \] Expanding and simplifying the terms, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{1}{|x|^3} \cdot \frac{2}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|^2} \cdot \frac{3}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|} \cdot \frac{1}{6} \] Multiplying this sum by \(x^3\) and considering the leading terms as \(x \to 0\): \[ x^3 \left( \frac{2}{6|x|^3} + \frac{3}{6|x|^2} + \frac{1}{6|x|} \right) = \frac{1}{3} + \frac{x}{2} + \frac{x^2}{6} \] As \(x \to 0\), the terms \(\frac{x}{2}\) and \(\frac{x^2}{6}\) approach 0, leaving the limit as: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{1}{3}} \]

The sum of squares from 1 to \(n\) is given by the formula: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \] Substituting \(n \approx \frac{1}{|x|}\) (since \(n\) is the integer part of \(\frac{1}{|x|}\) and the difference becomes negligible as \(x \to 0\)), we approximate the sum: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right)\left(\frac{1}{|x|} + 1\right)\left(2\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right) + 1\right)}{6} \] Expanding and simplifying the terms, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{1}{|x|^3} \cdot \frac{2}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|^2} \cdot \frac{3}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|} \cdot \frac{1}{6} \] Multiplying this sum by \(x^3\) and considering the leading terms as \(x \to 0\): \[ x^3 \left( \frac{2}{6|x|^3} + \frac{3}{6|x|^2} + \frac{1}{6|x|} \right) = \frac{1}{3} + \frac{x}{2} + \frac{x^2}{6} \] As \(x \to 0\), the terms \(\frac{x}{2}\) and \(\frac{x^2}{6}\) approach 0, leaving the limit as: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{1}{3}} \]

Let \(n = \left[\frac{1}{|x|}\right]\), which is the greatest integer less than or equal to \(\frac{1}{|x|}\). The sum of squares from 1 to \(n\) is given by the formula: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \] Substituting \(n \approx \frac{1}{|x|}\) (since \(n\) is the integer part of \(\frac{1}{|x|}\) and the difference becomes negligible as \(x \to 0\)), we approximate the sum: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right)\left(\frac{1}{|x|} + 1\right)\left(2\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right) + 1\right)}{6} \] Expanding and simplifying the terms, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{1}{|x|^3} \cdot \frac{2}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|^2} \cdot \frac{3}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|} \cdot \frac{1}{6} \] Multiplying this sum by \(x^3\) and considering the leading terms as \(x \to 0\): \[ x^3 \left( \frac{2}{6|x|^3} + \frac{3}{6|x|^2} + \frac{1}{6|x|} \right) = \frac{1}{3} + \frac{x}{2} + \frac{x^2}{6} \] As \(x \to 0\), the terms \(\frac{x}{2}\) and \(\frac{x^2}{6}\) approach 0, leaving the limit as: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{1}{3}} \]

Let \(n = \left[\frac{1}{|x|}\right]\), which is the greatest integer less than or equal to \(\frac{1}{|x|}\). The sum of squares from 1 to \(n\) is given by the formula: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \] Substituting \(n \approx \frac{1}{|x|}\) (since \(n\) is the integer part of \(\frac{1}{|x|}\) and the difference becomes negligible as \(x \to 0\)), we approximate the sum: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right)\left(\frac{1}{|x|} + 1\right)\left(2\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right) + 1\right)}{6} \] Expanding and simplifying the terms, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{1}{|x|^3} \cdot \frac{2}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|^2} \cdot \frac{3}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|} \cdot \frac{1}{6} \] Multiplying this sum by \(x^3\) and considering the leading terms as \(x \to 0\): \[ x^3 \left( \frac{2}{6|x|^3} + \frac{3}{6|x|^2} + \frac{1}{6|x|} \right) = \frac{1}{3} + \frac{x}{2} + \frac{x^2}{6} \] As \(x \to 0\), the terms \(\frac{x}{2}\) and \(\frac{x^2}{6}\) approach 0, leaving the limit as: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{1}{3}} \]

Let \(n = \left[\frac{1}{|x|}\right]\), which is the greatest integer less than or equal to \(\frac{1}{|x|}\). The sum of squares from 1 to \(n\) is given by the formula: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \] Substituting \(n \approx \frac{1}{|x|}\) (since \(n\) is the integer part of \(\frac{1}{|x|}\) and the difference becomes negligible as \(x \to 0\)), we approximate the sum: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right)\left(\frac{1}{|x|} + 1\right)\left(2\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right) + 1\right)}{6} \] Expanding and simplifying the terms, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{1}{|x|^3} \cdot \frac{2}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|^2} \cdot \frac{3}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|} \cdot \frac{1}{6} \] Multiplying this sum by \(x^3\) and considering the leading terms as \(x \to 0\): \[ x^3 \left( \frac{2}{6|x|^3} + \frac{3}{6|x|^2} + \frac{1}{6|x|} \right) = \frac{1}{3} + \frac{x}{2} + \frac{x^2}{6} \] As \(x \to 0\), the terms \(\frac{x}{2}\) and \(\frac{x^2}{6}\) approach 0, leaving the limit as: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{1}{3}} \]

Therefore, multiple approaches confirm that the limit is 1/3. **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{1}{3}} </think> To find the limit \(\lim \limits_{x \to 0} \ x^3\Big(1+2^2+3^2+\ldots+\Big[\frac{1}{\vert x \vert}\Big]^2\Big)\), we start by noting that as \(x \to 0\), \(\frac{1}{|x|}\) becomes very large. Let \(n = \left[\frac{1}{|x|}\right]\), which is the greatest integer less than or equal to \(\frac{1}{|x|}\). The sum of squares from 1 to \(n\) is given by the formula: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \] Substituting \(n \approx \frac{1}{|x|}\) (since \(n\) is the integer part of \(\frac{1}{|x|}\) and the difference becomes negligible as \(x \to 0\)), we approximate the sum: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right)\left(\frac{1}{|x|} + 1\right)\left(2\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right) + 1\right)}{6} \] Expanding and simplifying the terms, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{1}{|x|^3} \cdot \frac{2}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|^2} \cdot \frac{3}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|} \cdot \frac{1}{6} \] Multiplying this sum by \(x^3\) and considering the leading terms as \(x \to 0\): \[ x^3 \left( \frac{2}{6|x|^3} + \frac{3}{6|x|^2} + \frac{1}{6|x|} \right) = \frac{1}{3} + \frac{x}{2} + \frac{x^2}{6} \] As \(x \to 0\), the terms \(\frac{x}{2}\) and \(\frac{x^2}{6}\) approach 0, leaving the limit as: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{1}{3}} \]

Let \(n = \left[\frac{1}{|x|}\right]\), which is the greatest integer less than or equal to \(\frac{1}{|x|}\). The sum of squares from 1 to \(n\) is given by the formula: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \] Substituting \(n \approx \frac{1}{|x|}\) (since \(n\) is the integer part of \(\frac{1}{|x|}\) and the difference becomes negligible as \(x \to 0\)), we approximate the sum: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right)\left(\frac{1}{|x|} + 1\right)\left(2\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right) + 1\right)}{6} \] Expanding and simplifying the terms, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{1}{|x|^3} \cdot \frac{2}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|^2} \cdot \frac{3}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|} \cdot \frac{1}{6} \] Multiplying this sum by \(x^3\) and considering the leading terms as \(x \to 0\): \[ x^3 \left( \frac{2}{6|x|^3} + \frac{3}{6|x|^2} + \frac{1}{6|x|} \right) = \frac{1}{3} + \frac{x}{2} + \frac{x^2}{6} \] As \(x \to 0\), the terms \(\frac{x}{2}\) and \(\frac{x^2}{6}\) approach 0, leaving the limit as: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{1}{3}} \]

Let me expand the first two factors first: (1/x - δ)(1/x + 1 - δ) = (1/x)(1/x + 1 - δ) - δ(1/x + 1 - δ) = (1/x² + (1 - δ)/x) - (δ/x + δ(1 - δ)) = 1/x² + (1 - δ)/x - δ/x - δ(1 - δ) Simplify: 1/x² + [ (1 - δ) - δ ] /x - δ(1 - δ) = 1/x² + (1 - 2δ)/x - δ + δ² Now multiply this by the third factor (2/x + 1 - 2δ): [1/x² + (1 - 2δ)/x - δ + δ²] * [2/x + 1 - 2δ] Let's multiply term by term: First term: 1/x² * 2/x = 2/x³ Second term: 1/x² * (1 - 2δ) = (1 - 2δ)/x² Third term: (1 - 2δ)/x * 2/x = 2(1 - 2δ)/x² Fourth term: (1 - 2δ)/x * (1 - 2δ) = (1 - 2δ)^2 /x Fifth term: (-δ + δ²) * 2/x = (-2δ + 2δ²)/x Sixth term: (-δ + δ²) * (1 - 2δ) = -δ(1 - 2δ) + δ²(1 - 2δ) = -δ + 2δ² + δ² - 2δ³ = -δ + 3δ² - 2δ³ So combining all terms: 2/x³ + (1 - 2δ)/x² + 2(1 - 2δ)/x² + (1 - 2δ)^2 /x + (-2δ + 2δ²)/x + (-δ + 3δ² - 2δ³) Combine like terms: For 1/x³: 2/x³ For 1/x²: [ (1 - 2δ) + 2(1 - 2δ) ] /x² = [1 - 2δ + 2 - 4δ]/x² = (3 - 6δ)/x² For 1/x: [ (1 - 2δ)^2 + (-2δ + 2δ²) ] /x Let me compute (1 - 2δ)^2: = 1 - 4δ + 4δ² So adding (-2δ + 2δ²): 1 - 4δ + 4δ² -2δ + 2δ² = 1 - 6δ + 6δ² Therefore, the 1/x term is (1 - 6δ + 6δ²)/x Constant term: -δ + 3δ² - 2δ³ So overall, the product is: 2/x³ + (3 - 6δ)/x² + (1 - 6δ + 6δ²)/x + (-δ + 3δ² - 2δ³) Therefore, the sum is: [2/x³ + (3 - 6δ)/x² + (1 - 6δ + 6δ²)/x + (-δ + 3δ² - 2δ³)] /6 So when we multiply this by x³, the original expression becomes: x³ * Sum ≈ x³ * [2/(6x³) + (3 - 6δ)/(6x²) + (1 - 6δ + 6δ²)/(6x) + (-δ + 3δ² - 2δ³)/6] Simplify each term: First term: x³*(2)/(6x³) = 2/6 = 1/3 Second term: x³*(3 - 6δ)/(6x²) = (3 - 6δ)x/(6) = (1 - 2δ)x/2 Third term: x³*(1 - 6δ + 6δ²)/(6x) = (1 - 6δ + 6δ²)x²/6 Fourth term: x³*(-δ + 3δ² - 2δ³)/6 = (-δ + 3δ² - 2δ³)x³/6 Now, as x approaches 0, δ is between 0 and 1. So δ is bounded. Therefore, the second term is O(x), the third term is O(x²), and the fourth term is O(x³). All of these go to 0 as x approaches 0. Therefore, the limit is determined by the first term, which is 1/3. So the limit is 1/3? But wait, but δ is dependent on x. Since δ = 1/x - n, where n is [1/x], so δ = {1/x}, the fractional part of 1/x. So as x approaches 0, 1/x can be written as integer plus fractional part. So δ is between 0 and 1. However, as x approaches 0, depending on the sequence of x's approaching 0, δ can oscillate between 0 and 1. For example, if x approaches 0 through 1/n for integer n, then δ = 0. If x approaches 0 through 1/(n + 0.5), then δ = 0.5. So δ can vary. However, in our calculation above, the only term that could possibly depend on δ is the second term (1 - 2δ)x/2, but even though δ varies between 0 and 1, as x approaches 0, (1 - 2δ) is bounded (since δ is between 0 and1, 1 - 2δ is between -1 and1), so (1 - 2δ)x/2 approaches 0 as x approaches 0 regardless of δ. Similarly, the other terms also vanish. Therefore, regardless of the value of δ, the limit is 1/3. So maybe the answer is 1/3. But let me check with specific sequences. Suppose x approaches 0 through the sequence x_n = 1/n, where n is a positive integer. Then [1/x] = [n] = n. Then the sum is 1² + 2² + ... + n² = n(n + 1)(2n + 1)/6. Multiply by x³ = (1/n³): So expression is (1/n³) * [n(n + 1)(2n + 1)/6] = [n(n + 1)(2n + 1)]/(6n³) = [(n + 1)(2n + 1)]/(6n²) Expand numerator: (n + 1)(2n +1) = 2n² + 3n +1 Therefore, expression is (2n² + 3n +1)/(6n²) = (2 + 3/n + 1/n²)/6 As n approaches infinity, this approaches (2 + 0 +0)/6 = 2/6 = 1/3. So that's consistent. Now, take another sequence where x approaches 0 through x = 1/(n + 0.5), where n is integer. Then [1/x] = [n + 0.5] = n. So sum is still up to n, same as before. Then x = 1/(n + 0.5), so x³ = 1/(n + 0.5)^3, and the expression is [n(n +1)(2n +1)/6]/(n + 0.5)^3. Let me compute this as n approaches infinity. Let's write n + 0.5 as n(1 + 0.5/n). Then denominator is (n(1 + 0.5/n))³ = n³(1 + 0.5/n)^3 ≈ n³(1 + 1.5/n + ...) by binomial expansion. Numerator: n(n +1)(2n +1) ≈ n*(n)(2n) = 2n³. So numerator ≈2n³, denominator≈6n³. Therefore, expression ≈2n³/(6n³) = 1/3. The higher order terms would be lower order in n, so as n approaches infinity, the expression still approaches 1/3. So regardless of how x approaches 0, the limit seems to be 1/3. But let me test with x approaching 0 through some irrational sequence. For example, x = 1/(n + sqrt(2)), but here [1/x] would still be n, as 1/x = n + sqrt(2), so [1/x] = n. So similar to the previous case, the sum would be up to n, and x³ = 1/(n + sqrt(2))³. Then the expression is [n(n +1)(2n +1)/6]/(n + sqrt(2))³. As n approaches infinity, the same reasoning applies: numerator ~2n³, denominator ~6n³, so limit 1/3. Therefore, seems like regardless of the approach path, the limit is 1/3. But let me check with another example where x approaches 0 in a different way, say x = 1/(n + ε), where ε is a fixed between 0 and1. For instance, ε=0.1. Then [1/x] = n, sum is up to n, x³ =1/(n +0.1)^3. Then expression is [n(n +1)(2n +1)/6]/(n +0.1)^3. Again, as n→∞, numerator ~2n³, denominator ~6n³, so expression ~1/3. Alternatively, take x approaching 0 through x=1/(n - 0.1). Then [1/x] = [n -0.1] = n -1. Wait, but 1/x =n -0.1, so [1/x] = n -1. Then sum is up to n -1. So expression is [(n -1)n(2n -1)/6]/(n -0.1)^3. Let's compute this: Numerator: (n -1)n(2n -1) ≈ (n² -n)(2n -1) ≈2n³ -n² -2n² +n ≈2n³ -3n² +n ~2n³ Denominator:6(n -0.1)^3 ≈6n³(1 -0.1/n)^3 ≈6n³(1 -0.3/n + ...) ~6n³ Thus, the expression ~2n³/(6n³)=1/3. So still converges to 1/3. Therefore, regardless of how x approaches 0, as long as we take the limit, the expression tends to 1/3. But wait, in the initial approximation, we neglected the δ term, assuming that n ≈1/x. But even when we considered δ, the correction terms ended up vanishing in the limit. Therefore, the limit is indeed 1/3. Alternatively, perhaps we can use integral approximation for the sum. The sum from k=1 to n of k² is approximately the integral from 0 to n of t² dt, which is n³/3. But the sum is actually larger than the integral. The integral from 0 to n of t² dt = n³/3, and the sum from k=1 to n of k² is ~n³/3 + n²/2 + n/6. So the leading term is n³/3. So when we multiply by x³, and since n = [1/x] ≈1/x, the leading term is (1/x)³/3 *x³ =1/3, which matches our previous result. The next term would be n²/2 ~ (1/x²)/2 *x³ =x/2, which tends to 0. So this also suggests that the limit is 1/3. Therefore, multiple approaches confirm that the limit is 1/3. **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{1}{3}} </think> To find the limit \(\lim \limits_{x \to 0} \ x^3\Big(1+2^2+3^2+\ldots+\Big[\frac{1}{\vert x \vert}\Big]^2\Big)\), we start by noting that as \(x \to 0\), \(\frac{1}{|x|}\) becomes very large. Let \(n = \left[\frac{1}{|x|}\right]\), which is the greatest integer less than or equal to \(\frac{1}{|x|}\). The sum of squares from 1 to \(n\) is given by the formula: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \] Substituting \(n \approx \frac{1}{|x|}\) (since \(n\) is the integer part of \(\frac{1}{|x|}\) and the difference becomes negligible as \(x \to 0\)), we approximate the sum: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right)\left(\frac{1}{|x|} + 1\right)\left(2\left(\frac{1}{|x|}\right) + 1\right)}{6} \] Expanding and simplifying the terms, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{n} k^2 \approx \frac{1}{|x|^3} \cdot \frac{2}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|^2} \cdot \frac{3}{6} + \frac{1}{|x|} \cdot \frac{1}{6} \] Multiplying this sum by \(x^3\) and considering the leading terms as \(x \to 0\): \[ x^3 \left( \frac{2}{6|x|^3} + \frac{3}{6|x|^2} + \frac{1}{6|x|} \right) = \frac{1}{3} + \frac{x}{2} + \frac{x^2}{6} \] As \(x \to 0\), the terms \(\frac{x}{2}\) and \(\frac{x^2}{6}\) approach 0, leaving the limit as: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{1}{3}} \]
